Delegation and Change of Intention in Expiation
Ruling with proof: delegation in expiation and intention at ta'liq

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on changing intention in delegated expiation and explain why intention at the time of a conditional statement (ta'liq) is valid despite the condition not being a cause at the moment of utterance.

Core Idea
Intention can be changed before an agent acts, and intention at ta'liq is valid because the condition is a potential cause.

This lesson addresses two interconnected issues in the context of expiation: delegation of manumission and the timing of intention. Using analogies from the rules of ihsar and charity, the Hanafi school establishes that a principal may rescind and redirect his intention before the agent executes the act. Additionally, the passage resolves a seeming contradiction: although a conditional statement (mu'allaq) is not a cause of manumission until the condition occurs, intention at the time of uttering the condition is still considered valid. This is explained by treating the condition as a 'potential cause' (sabab muqaddar), a nuance that prevents unnecessary hardship.

Guided Path
1
Understand the delegation scenario
A person authorizes an agent to free his slave for expiation. Before the agent acts, the principal changes his intention, wanting the freeing to serve another expiation (e.g., a second broken oath). The text rules this permissible.
The agent's act has not yet occurred, so the principal's intention can still be redirected.
2
Identify the proof via analogy
The ruling is derived by analogy (qiyas) from three cases: (a) a person prevented from Hajj (muhsar) sends a sacrificial animal, then later becomes free and intends it for a new ihsar; (b) a person gives food to an agent for charity for oath expiation, then before the agent acts, the principal expiates another oath and intends the charity for that; (c) a person sends a compensation animal for hunting, then becomes muhsar and intends it for ihsar. In all, change of intention before execution is valid.
The analogy is based on the common factor that the act (freeing, sacrificing, giving charity) is not yet performed.
3
Analyze the problematic point on mu'allaq
The author notes a contradiction: Hanafi principles state that a conditional statement (e.g., 'if I enter the house, my slave is free for expiation') does not become a cause until the condition occurs. Therefore, intention at the time of uttering the condition should not be valid—it should only be valid when the condition is fulfilled. Yet the ruling validates intention at ta'liq.
This is the central theoretical tension the passage resolves.
4
Locate the resolution from Fath al-Qadir
The answer in Fath al-Qadir (from the chapter on oaths) is that the conditional statement is considered a 'potential cause' (sabab muqaddar). Therefore, intention at the time of utterance is valid because it is directed toward this potential cause, not the actual future event. This aligns with the school's position that intention accompanies the cause, even if the cause is not yet fully effective.
This resolution preserves both the principle and the practical ruling.
5
Distinguish the umm walad case
The passage ends with a contrasting example: buying one's own umm walad (slave mother of his child) with the intention that it serve as his expiation is invalid. Why? Because the cause of manumission here is istilad (the birth of the child), not the purchase. The intention to free for expiation did not accompany that cause (istilad), so the condition is not met.
This shows that intention must accompany the actual cause, not a separate act.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم اعلم أنه لو وكل في إعتاقه عبده عن كفارته ثم نوى قبل إعتاق المأمور أن يكون عن جهة أخرى فإنه يجوز فهماً من كلام المحيط من باب الإحصار لو بعث المحصر بهدي الاحصار ثم زال وحدث آخر فإن علم أن يدرك الهدى ونوى أن يكون لإحصاره الثاني جاز، وكذا لو دفع خمسة أصوع طعام لرجل وأمره بالتصدق على عشرة مساكين عن كفارة يمينه فلم يتصدق حتى كفر الأمر وحنث في آخرى ثم تصدق المأمور جاز عن الثانية إذا نواها الأمر، وكذا لو بعث هدياً الجزاء صيد ثم أحصر فنوى أن يكون للإحصار، ولو قلد بدنة وأوجبها تطوعاً ثم أحصر فنوى أن تكون لإحصاره جاز ا هـ . ثم اعلم أنهم جعلوا المعلق هنا علة للعتق مع قولهم إن المعلق لا ينعقد سبباً للحال وإنما ينعقد سبباً عند وجود الشرط فينبغي على هذا الأصل أن لا تصح النية وقت التعليق وإنما تصح وقت وجود الشرط والحكم فيها بالعكس وجوابه في فتح القدير من كتاب الأيمان من باب اليمين في الطلاق والعتاق، وقد ذكروا فيه أنه لو اشترى أم ولده أي من استولدها بنكاح ناوياً عن كفارته فإنه لا يجوز لأن العدة الاستيلاد ولم تقارنه النية.
Then know that if he authorized someone to free his slave for his expiation, then intended before the agent's freeing that it be for another cause, it is permissible, understood from the words of al-Muhit from the chapter of ihsar: if the confined person sent the sacrificial animal of ihsar, then his state ended and another occurred, and he knows that he can catch the animal and intends it for his second ihsar, it is permissible. Similarly, if he gave five sa' of food to a man and ordered him to give charity to ten poor people for the expiation of his oath, and he did not give charity until the principal expiated and broke another oath, then the agent gave charity, it is permissible for the second if the principal intended it. Similarly, if he sent a sacrificial animal for compensation for hunting, then became confined and intended it for ihsar, it is permissible. And if he garlanded a camel and made it obligatory as a voluntary offering, then became confined and intended it for his ihsar, it is permissible. Then know that they made the conditional statement here a cause for manumission despite their saying that the conditional does not become a cause at present, but becomes a cause upon existence of the condition. According to this principle, intention at the time of condition should not be valid, but only at the time of fulfillment, yet the ruling is opposite. The answer is in Fath al-Qadir from the book of oaths, chapter of divorce and manumission; and they mentioned therein that if he bought his umm walad (slave mother of his child) i.e., one he had made pregnant through valid marriage, intending it for his expiation, it is not permitted because the cause is istilad (producing a child) and intention did not accompany it.
Source pages p. 174

Commentary
This passage presents two additional issues: (1) Delegation in freeing a slave for expiation: the principal may change his intention before the agent acts, based on analogies from ihsar (being prevented from Hajj) and charity. (2) The paradoxical ruling that intention at the time of a conditional statement (ta'liq) is valid even though the statement is not a cause until the condition occurs; the resolution from Fath al-Qadir is that the condition is considered a 'potential cause,' so intention at the time of condition is valid. The passage also notes that buying one's own umm walad (slave mother of his child) with intention of expiation is invalid because the cause of manumission is the birth of the child (istilad), not the purchase, and intention did not accompany that cause.

Key Terms
توكيل (tawkīl): Delegation of action (in this case, freeing a slave) to an agent.
معلق (mu'allaq): A conditional statement that becomes effective upon fulfillment of the condition.
إحصار (ihsār): Being prevented from completing Hajj or Umrah due to enemy or illness.
أم ولد (umm walad): A slave woman who has borne a child to her master through valid marriage or concubinage.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a person authorizes an agent to free his slave for expiation, and before the agent acts, the principal intends it for a different expiation, what is the ruling?
A) Invalid because the intention was fixed at authorization.
B) Valid because the principal can change intention before actual manumission.
C) Valid only if the agent also intends the change.
D) Invalid because the slave is already designated.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, a principal may change his intention in delegated expiation before the agent acts, and intention at the time of a conditional statement is valid because the condition is treated as a potential cause.

Freeing Half a Slave for Expiation and Completing It
Ruling with proof on freeing half a slave for expiation and completing it

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on freeing half a slave for expiation and determine when completing the manumission is valid or invalid according to Abu Hanifa.

Core Idea
Freeing half then the other half of the same slave counts as one complete manumission; mixing halves from different slaves does not.

This lesson examines a specific case from the matn: a person who frees half his slave as expiation and later frees the remaining half before consummation. The commentator explains the validity of this act and restricts it to freeing the same slave, clarifying that using a different slave or mixing with feeding is invalid. We will break down the logical steps and the underlying principle.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
The act is freeing half a slave for expiation, then freeing the remaining half before consummation. The ruling is that this is valid and constitutes freeing one complete slave.
The ruling depends on the remainder being freed before the act requiring expiation.
2
Understand the analogy
The commentator compares this case to a sheep for sacrifice whose eye is accidentally hit by the knife; a minor defect does not invalidate the sacrifice. Similarly, the fact that the slave was freed in two statements does not invalidate the expiation.
The analogy shows that a minor procedural imperfection does not prevent validity.
3
Identify the restriction
The ruling is restricted to freeing the same slave's other half. If one frees half from another slave, it is invalid because the requirement is a single complete slave from one source.
This is the position of Abu Hanifa as stated in the text.
4
Note the exception for mixed methods
Similarly, combining a half-freeing with feeding (e.g., freeing half and feeding for the rest) is also invalid because neither a complete slave nor a full feeding has been performed.
The expiation must be fulfilled entirely by one of the specified methods.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الثالث أعني ما إذا حرر نصف عبده ثم حرر باقيه قبل المسيس فلونه أعتق رقبة كاملة بكلامين والنقصان متمكن على ملكه التحرير عنها ومثله غير مانع كمن أضجع شاة للأضحية فأصابت السكين عينها. قيد بقوله حرر باقيه لأنه لو حرر نصفاً آخر من رقبة أخرى لا يجوز فلا يجوز تكميل العتق بالعتق من شخص آخر عند أبي حنيفة وأما تكميله بالإطعام كما لو حرر عنها نصف عبد وأطعم عن الباقي لم يجز أيضاً عند أبي حنيفة لأنها إنما تتأدى بإعتاق رقبة أو بإطعام مساكين مقدرة ولم يوجد واحد منهما وتكميل العتق بالعتق من شخص آخر لا يجوز فلأن لا يجوز تكميله بسبب
As for the third, i.e., if he freed half of his slave then freed the remainder of him before contact, then it is as if he freed a complete slave in two statements, and the deficiency is present in his ownership at the time of freeing for it, and something like it is not a preventer, like one who laid down a sheep for sacrifice and the knife hit its eye. He restricted by his saying 'freed the remainder' because if he freed another half from another slave, it is not permissible, for completion of manumission by manumission from another person is not permissible according to Abu Hanifa. As for completing it by feeding, e.g., if he freed half a slave for it and fed for the rest, it is also not permissible according to Abu Hanifa because it is only fulfilled by manumission of a slave or by feeding a specified number of poor, and neither one has been fully achieved. And completion of manumission by manumission from another person is not permissible, so it is not permissible to complete it by [that] cause.
Source pages p. 174

Commentary
This passage addresses the third case in the matn: freeing half a slave for expiation and then freeing the other half. The commentator explains that this is valid because the combined acts result in a complete slave being freed in two statements, and the fact that the slave was partially owned (the freed half was part of the same slave) does not prevent validity, analogous to a sacrificial animal that has a minor defect. However, the commentator restricts the ruling to freeing the same slave's other half; if one frees half from two different slaves, it is invalid according to Abu Hanifa, because the expiation requires freeing one complete slave. Also, mixing a half-freeing with feeding for the remaining half is invalid. The text cuts off but likely continues with the reasoning.

Key Terms
رقبة (raqaba): A complete slave (the whole person).
تكميل العتق (takmīl al-'itq): Completing the manumission by freeing the remaining part of a partially freed slave.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, can a person free half a slave for expiation and then free half from another slave to complete the expiation?
A) Yes, because the total equals one complete slave.
B) No, because manumission cannot be completed from a different slave.
C) Yes, only if the two halves are from the same owner.
D) No, except if the first half is from a close relative.

Key Takeaway
Freeing half then the other half of the same slave for expiation is valid and counts as one complete manumission; freeing halves from different slaves or mixing with feeding is invalid.

Intention at Ta'liq: The Binding Rule
Ruling with proof from al-Bada'i' and al-Tatarakhaniyyah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state and apply the Hanafi rule that intention at the time of a conditional statement (ta'liq) determines the purpose of the consequent act, and distinguish it from cases where intention can be changed.

Core Idea
Intention at ta'liq is binding — the original purpose prevails over later changes.

When a man makes a conditional emancipation for expiation, the intention he holds at the moment of the condition governs the act. Even if he later wishes to repurpose the emancipation for a different expiation, the original intent stands. This lesson unpacks the reasoning behind this rule and its application in zihar cases.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its legal category
Recognise that the act is a conditional emancipation (ta'liq) linked to a future event (e.g., buying a slave). The intention at ta'liq determines the legal nature (wajib expiation vs voluntary).
The ruling applies to any expiation (kaffara) or voluntary offering.
2
State the Hanafi ruling
The intention at the time of ta'liq is binding. If he intends it for zihar, it remains for zihar; if for voluntary, it cannot later become expiation.
This is based on the maxim 'al-manawi ka al-malfuz bihi' (the intended is like the uttered).
3
Understand the underlying reason
The conditional statement is considered a potential cause; once uttered, it cannot be rescinded because the intention is as if spoken. Hence, later changes are ineffective.
This contrasts with cases where the intention can change, such as when an agent is involved.
4
Apply to the given example
If he says 'If I buy this slave, he is free for my zihar' then later intends him for killing expiation, it counts for zihar. Similarly, if he intends voluntary initially, later intention for expiation is invalid.
The examples from al-Bada'i' and al-Tatarakhaniyyah illustrate multiple intentions.
5
Note the exception: agency
If he appoints an agent to free the slave, he can change the intention before the agent acts. This is an exception derived from analogy with ihsar.
This will be covered in the next lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بقوله عن ظهاري أو ينوي، فلو نوى وقت التعليق أن يكون حراً عن ظهاره ثم نوى أن يكون عن كفارة قتله كان عن الظهار، وكذا لو نوى وقته أن يكون تطوعاً ثم نوى عنها لم يصح؛ كذا في البدائع معللاً بأن اليمين لا يحتمل الفسخ بناء على أن المنوي كالملفوظ به . . وفي التتارخانية : وعلى هذا لو قال إن اشتريت هذا العبد فهو حر عن ظهاري ثم قال إن اشتريته فهو حر عن ظهار فلانة ثم قال لامرأة أخرى كذلك ثم اشتراه فهو حر عن ظهار الأولى ا اهـ. ثم اعلم أنه لو وكل في إعتاقه عبده عن كفارته ثم نوى قبل إعتاق المأمور أن يكون عن جهة أخرى فإنه يجوز فهماً من كلام المحيط من باب الإحصار لو بعث المحصر بهدي الاحصار ثم زال وحدث آخر فإن علم أن يدرك الهدى ونوى أن يكون لإحصاره الثاني جاز، وكذا لو دفع خمسة أصوع طعام لرجل وأمره بالتصدق على عشرة مساكين عن كفارة يمينه فلم يتصدق حتى كفر الأمر وحنث في آخرى ثم تصدق المأمور جاز عن الثانية إذا نواها الأمر، وكذا لو بعث هدياً الجزاء صيد ثم أحصر فنوى أن يكون للإحصار، ولو قلد بدنة وأوجبها تطوعاً ثم أحصر فنوى أن تكون لإحصاره جاز ا هـ . ثم اعلم أنهم جعلوا المعلق هنا علة للعتق مع قولهم إن المعلق لا ينعقد سبباً للحال وإنما ينعقد سبباً عند وجود الشرط فينبغي على هذا الأصل أن لا تصح النية وقت التعليق وإنما تصح وقت وجود الشرط والحكم فيها بالعكس وجوابه في فتح القدير من كتاب الأيمان من باب اليمين في الطلاق والعتاق، وقد ذكروا فيه أنه لو اشترى أم ولده أي من استولدها بنكاح ناوياً عن كفارته فإنه لا يجوز لأن العدة الاستيلاد ولم تقارنه النية . وأما الثالث أعني ما إذا حرر نصف عبده ثم حرر باقيه قبل المسيس فلونه أعتق رقبة كاملة بكلامين والنقصان متمكن على ملكه التحرير عنها ومثله غير مانع كمن أضجع شاة للأضحية فأصابت السكين عينها. قيد بقوله حرر باقية» لأنه لو حرر نصفاً آخر من رقبة أخرى لا يجوز فلا يجوز تكميل العتق بالعتق من شخص آخر عند أبي حنيفة وأما تكميله بالإطعام كما لو حرر عنها نصف عبد وأطعم عن الباقي لم يجز أيضاً عند أبي حنيفة لأنها إنما تتأدى بإعتاق رقبة أو بإطعام مساكين مقدرة ولم يوجد واحد منهما وتكميل العتق بالعتق من شخص آخر لا يجوز فلأن لا يجوز تكميله بسبب
By his statement about zihar or intention: if he intended at the time of the conditional statement that it be free for his zihar, then later intended that it be for expiation of killing, it counts for the zihar. Similarly, if he intended at its time that it be voluntary, then later intended for it, it is not valid, as in al-Bada'i', reasoned that the oath cannot be rescinded because the intended is like the uttered. In al-Tatarakhaniyyah: on this, if he said 'If I buy this slave, he is free for my zihar', then said 'If I buy him, he is free for the zihar of so-and-so', then said to another woman likewise, then bought him, he is free for the first zihar, etc. Then know that if he appointed an agent to free his slave for his expiation, then before the agent frees, he intends it for another purpose, it is permissible, as understood from al-Muhit from the chapter of ihsar: if the muhsar sent a hadi of ihsar then the obstacle ceased and another occurred, if he knows that the hadi can be reached and intends it for his second ihsar, it is permitted. Similarly, if he gave five sa's of food to a man and ordered him to give charity to ten poor persons for the expiation of his oath, and he did not give until the one who ordered committed another oath and broke it, then the agent gave charity, it is permitted for the second if the one who ordered intended it. Similarly, if he sent a hadi as compensation for game, then became muhsar, and intended it for (the new) ihsar, it is permitted. And if he garlanded a badanah and made it obligatory as a voluntary offering, then became muhsar and intended it for his ihsar, it is permitted, etc. Then know that they made the conditional here a cause for emancipation despite their statement that the conditional does not become a cause immediately, but only becomes a cause when the condition occurs. So according to this principle, the intention at the time of ta'liq should not be valid, but only at the time of the condition's existence, yet the ruling is the opposite. The answer to this is in Fath al-Qadir from the book of oaths, chapter on oaths in divorce and emancipation. They mentioned therein that if he bought the umm walad of his – i.e., the one he had a child with through marriage – intending it for his expiation, it is not valid because the waiting period of istilad (did not elapse?) and intention did not accompany it. As for the third, i.e., if he freed half his slave then freed the rest before cohabitation, he has freed a complete slave with two utterances, and the deficiency was present in his ownership at the time of freeing, and the like does not prevent, like one who lays down a sheep for sacrifice and the knife hits its eye. He restricted it with 'freed its remainder' because if he freed another half from another slave, it is not valid; so completing emancipation through emancipation from another person is not allowed according to Abu Hanifah. As for completing it through feeding, such as if he freed half a slave for it and fed for the remainder, it is also not allowed according to Abu Hanifah because it is only fulfilled by emancipating a slave or by feeding a specified number of poor persons, and neither occurred. So completing emancipation through emancipation from another person is not allowed, so that it is not allowed to complete it by means of (another).
Source pages p. 174

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq deals with the conditions of intention (niyyah) in expiation (kaffara) for zihar. The author clarifies that the intention at the time of the conditional statement (ta'liq) is what matters, not any subsequent change. For example, if a man makes an oath conditional on buying a slave, saying 'If I buy this slave, he is free for my zihar,' then later changes his mind and wants it for another expiation, the original intention prevails because the conditional oath is like an utterance that cannot be rescinded. The same applies if he initially intends it as a voluntary act but later wants it for expiation; it does not become expiation because the intention at the time of ta'liq is binding. The author then discusses the case of appointing an agent to free a slave for expiation. If the principal changes his intention before the agent acts, the new intention is valid. This is derived by analogy from the rules of ihsar (being prevented from completing Hajj) where a person can redirect a sacrificial animal from one purpose to another before it is sacrificed. The passage then addresses a theoretical objection: since a conditional statement does not become a cause until the condition occurs, why should intention at the time of ta'liq be considered? The answer, from Fath al-Qadir, is that the conditional statement is considered a potential cause, and thus intention at that time is considered, just as sanity at the time of ta'liq is required for validity even though insanity at the time of condition does not prevent enforcement. The author also mentions that buying one's own umm walad (a slave who bore his child) cannot fulfill an expiation because the intention was not present at the moment of acquisition due to the waiting period element. Finally, the 'third issue' introduces the rule that freeing half a slave and then the remainder before cohabitation (in zihar) constitutes a complete emancipated slave because the deficiency was in the owner's control and does not prevent validity, similar to a sheep that accidentally becomes defective during sacrifice.

Key Terms
التعليق (al-ta'liq): A conditional statement linking the occurrence of a legal effect (like emancipation) to a future condition.
النية (al-niyyah): The intention of the heart that accompanies a legal act, determining its purpose (e.g., for expiation or voluntary).
الظهار (al-zihar): A form of divorce-like statement where a husband compares his wife to a female relative, requiring expiation before marital relations.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a man intends at ta'liq that his slave be free for zihar, but later before the condition occurs, he intends it for the expiation of a broken oath, what is the ruling?
A) The emancipation counts for the broken oath because later intention overrides.
B) The emancipation counts for the zihar because intention at ta'liq is binding.
C) The emancipation is invalid because intention changed.
D) It counts for both if he intended both.

Key Takeaway
Intention at the moment of a conditional statement determines the purpose of the consequent act and cannot be changed later, except when an agent is involved.

Agency and Change of Intention: Analogy from Ihsar
Ruling with proof from al-Muhit and Fath al-Qadir, expanding via qiyas.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that when an agent is appointed to free a slave for expiation, the principal can change the intention before the agent acts, using the analogy from ihsar.

Core Idea
An agent's act is attributed to the principal's current intention, not the original ta'liq intention.

The previous lesson established that intention at ta'liq is binding. However, when a principal appoints an agent to execute the emancipation, the ruling changes: the principal can repurpose the expiation before the agent acts. This lesson explores the reasoning via qiyas from the rules of ihsar (being prevented from completing Hajj) and other examples, and addresses a theoretical objection about the timing of intention.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario with an agent
The principal appoints an agent to free a slave for a specific expiation. Later, before the agent frees, the principal changes his intention to a different expiation.
The agent has not yet performed the act.
2
State the ruling: change is permitted
The principal can change the purpose because the agent's act is still attributable to the principal's current intention. The original ta'liq intention does not control.
This is an exception to the binding rule from the previous lesson.
3
Understand the analogy from ihsar
Just as a person performing Hajj who becomes muhsar (prevented) can redirect a sacrificial animal (hadi) from one ihsar to another before it is slaughtered by an agent, so too can a principal redirect the emancipation.
The qiyas is based on the common element: both involve an agent's act that is not yet concluded.
4
Extend to other examples
The text gives parallel cases: giving food for charity on behalf of an expiation, then redirecting it before the agent distributes; sending a hadi for game compensation and then redirecting it; or dedicating a badanah voluntarily and then redirecting it for ihsar.
These reinforce the principle that the principal's current intention controls until the agent performs.
5
Address the theoretical objection
The passage notes an apparent contradiction: if the conditional only becomes a cause when the condition occurs, why is intention at ta'liq considered? The answer from Fath al-Qadir: the conditional is a potential cause, and intention at that moment is considered just as sanity at ta'liq is required.
This resolves the objection and harmonises the rules.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Key Terms
التعليق (al-ta'liq): A conditional statement linking the occurrence of a legal effect (like emancipation) to a future condition.
النيابَة (al-niyabah): Agency or deputyship, where one person (the principal) appoints another (the agent) to perform a legal act on his behalf.
الإحصار (al-ihsar): Being prevented from completing Hajj or Umrah due to enemy, illness, or other obstacles, requiring a sacrificial animal (hadi).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A man appoints an agent to free his slave for the expiation of a broken oath. Before the agent frees, the man decides to use the slave for a zihar expiation. What is the ruling?
A) The agent must follow the original instructions; the man must fulfill zihar separately.
B) The man can change the intention, so the emancipation counts for the zihar.
C) The man can change the intention, but only if the agent agrees.
D) The emancipation is invalid because the purpose must be clear at the start.

Key Takeaway
When an agent is appointed, the principal can change the intention before the agent executes the act, based on qiyas from the rules of ihsar.

Freeing Half Your Own Slave: A Valid Path to Complete Kaffara
Ruling on freeing half then rest of own slave

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions under which freeing half a slave and then the rest fulfills kaffara, and apply the qiyas analogy to distinguish it from other scenarios.

Core Idea
Freeing half then rest of the same slave counts as a full emancipation for kaffara because the deficiency was in your ownership.

This lesson examines the third scenario discussed in al-Bahr al-Ra'iq: a person who frees half his slave and then the remaining half before cohabitation. We will see why this is valid, based on the principle that the deficiency was within his ownership at the time of freeing, and the analogy to a sacrificial sheep that becomes slightly defective during slaughter.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the ruling
The act is freeing half a slave and then the remainder before having marital relations. The ruling: this constitutes a valid complete emancipation for kaffara.
This is according to Abu Hanifa and agreed upon.
2
Locate the qiyas analogy
The text compares this to laying down a sheep for sacrifice and the knife hitting its eye. Just as the defect during the process does not invalidate the sacrifice, the deficiency in ownership (half unfreed) during the process does not prevent it from being a full slave.
The qiyas is jaliy (clear).
3
Understand the condition
The condition is that the second freeing must be of the same slave's remaining half, not a different slave and not mixing with feeding.
If you free half from one slave and half from another, it is not valid.
4
Distinguish from jointly owned slave
This scenario is distinct from the case of a jointly owned slave, which we will cover in the next lesson. Here the deficiency was within your ownership; in the joint case it is not.
This sets the stage for the intra-Hanafi dispute.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الثالث أعني ما إذا حرر نصف عبده ثم حرر باقيه قبل المسيس فلونه أعتق رقبة كاملة بكلامين والنقصان متمكن على ملكه التحرير عنها ومثله غير مانع كمن أضجع شاة للأضحية فأصابت السكين عينها. قيد بقوله حرر باقية» لأنه لو حرر نصفاً آخر من رقبة أخرى لا يجوز فلا يجوز تكميل العتق بالعتق من شخص آخر عند أبي حنيفة وأما تكميله بالإطعام كما لو حرر عنها نصف عبد وأطعم عن الباقي لم يجز أيضاً عند أبي حنيفة لأنها إنما تتأدى بإعتاق رقبة أو بإطعام مساكين مقدرة ولم يوجد واحد منهما وتكميل العتق بالعتق من شخص آخر لا يجوز فلأن لا يجوز تكميله بسبب قال إن اشتريته فهو حر عن ظهار (فلانة ساقط من بعض النسخ وهو موجود في التتارخانية قوله : (ثم اعلم أنه لو وكل في اعتاقه الخ) نقله المقدسي في شرحه حازماً به قوله : (وجوابه في فتح القدير الخ) نقله المقدسي في شرحه وهو أنه لما كان قبل الشرط بعرضيته أن يصير علة اعتبر له حكم العلة حتى اعتبرت الأهلية عنده اتفاقاً، فلو كان مجنوناً عند وقوع الشرط وقع الطلاق والعتاق، ولو كان مجنوناً عند التعليق لم يعتبر أصلاً فلذا يجب تعتبر النية عنده .
As for the third, that is, if he freed half his slave then freed the rest before cohabitation, then he has freed a complete slave with two utterances, and the deficiency was present in his ownership at the time of freeing, and the like does not prevent, like one who lays down a sheep for sacrifice and the knife hits its eye. He restricted it with 'freed its remainder' because if he freed another half from another slave, it is not valid. So completing emancipation through emancipation from another person is not allowed according to Abu Hanifah. And as for completing it through feeding, such as if he freed half a slave for it and fed for the remainder, it is also not allowed according to Abu Hanifah, because it is only fulfilled by emancipating a slave or by feeding a specified number of poor persons, and neither occurred. So completing emancipation through emancipation from another person is not allowed, so that it is not allowed to complete it by means of (another). He said: If I buy him, he is free from zihar (so-and-so is missing in some copies, but present in al-Tatarakhaniyyah). Then know that if he appointed an agent in freeing his slave for his expiation, etc., al-Maqdisi transmitted it in his commentary with certainty. And his saying: (And the answer is in Fath al-Qadir, etc.) al-Maqdisi transmitted it in his commentary, and it is that since it is before the condition, by its potentiality to become a cause, the ruling of the cause is considered for it, so the capacity was considered with him unanimously. So if he was insane at the time of the condition's occurrence, divorce and emancipation occur, and if he was insane at the time of ta'liq, it is not considered at all. Therefore, intention must be considered with him.
Source pages p. 174

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq addresses the third issue concerning expiation (kaffara) for zihar: whether freeing half a slave and then the other half constitutes a valid expiation. The author explains that according to Imam Abu Hanifah, if a man frees half of his own slave and then frees the remaining half before having marital relations with his wife (in the case of zihar), it is valid and counts as freeing a complete slave. The reason is that the deficiency (the half being unfree) was within his ownership at the time of the first freeing and does not prevent it from being considered a full slave, similar to the case where a sacrificial sheep becomes slightly defective during slaughter. However, this ruling is restricted to the case where the second freeing is of the same slave's remaining half. If a person frees a half from one slave and then another half from a different slave, it does not complete a full slave for kaffara because the emancipation must be of one specific individual. Similarly, mixing emancipation with feeding (e.g., freeing half a slave and feeding the remaining expiation) is not valid according to Abu Hanifah, because the obligation is to either free a complete slave or feed sixty poor persons, not a combination of both. The passage then mentions a dispute between Abu Hanifah and the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) regarding a jointly owned slave. If a person frees his share of a jointly owned slave and then guarantees (i.e., pays the value of) the other share, according to Abu Hanifah, this does not suffice for kaffara because the other half was not freed from his own ownership; the deficiency (the other partner's share) was not within his control at the time of freeing. But according to the Sahibayn, if he is wealthy and guarantees the value, it suffices because they hold that emancipation is not divisible (la yatajazzau) and thus freeing the share frees the whole slave. The text also discusses the underlying principle: the impediment is that the slave incurs a debt (the value to the partner) which contradicts the purity required for expiation. The marginal notes indicate that al-Maqdisi transmitted the answer from Fath al-Qadir regarding the time of intention, emphasizing that capacity (ahlīyah) is considered at the time of ta'liq because the conditional statement is considered a potential cause, analogous to sanity being required at the moment of the condition's occurrence.

Key Terms
العتق (al-'itq): Emancipation of a slave; in kaffara, it must be a complete, unencumbered slave.
الكفارة (al-kaffarah): Expiation required for breaking an oath, zihar, or other violations; options include freeing a slave, fasting, or feeding the poor.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, freeing half a slave and then the remainder from the same slave is valid for kaffara because:
A) The deficiency was in his ownership at the time.
B) Emancipation is not divisible.
C) He can pay the value later.
D) The slave must be freed completely from the beginning.

Key Takeaway
Freeing half then the rest of the same slave before cohabitation constitutes a full emancipation for kaffara because the deficiency was within the owner's control.

Partial Emancipation of a Joint Slave: Dispute between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn
Intra-Hanafi dispute on jointly owned slave for kaffara

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the intra-Hanafi dispute between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn regarding partial emancipation of a jointly owned slave for kaffara, and apply the tarjih to determine the mufta bihi.

Core Idea
Abu Hanifa rejects a jointly owned slave for kaffara due to encumbrance; Sahibayn accept if wealthy and guarantee.

This lesson delves into the disputed scenario: a person who owns a half share of a slave and then guarantees the other half. The Imams differ based on whether emancipation is divisible and whether a resulting debt invalidates the kaffara. Understanding this dispute sharpens your ability to distinguish between different types of deficiency and their legal effect.

Guided Path
1
State the scenario
A person owes kaffara and owns half a slave; he frees his share and then guarantees (pays the value) to the co-owner to free the rest.
This is a common situation when a slave is jointly inherited or purchased.
2
Present Abu Hanifa's position
Abu Hanifa says it is not valid because the deficiency (the other half) was not in his ownership at the time, and the slave becomes burdened with debt (the value guaranteed), which defeats the purpose of kaffara.
He emphasizes the purity of the slave and the owner's control.
3
Present the Sahibayn's position
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad say it is valid if he is wealthy and guarantees the value, because emancipation is not divisible (al-'itq la yatajazzau) so freeing his share automatically frees the whole; the guarantee is just compensation.
They focus on the nature of emancipation itself.
4
Compare the proofs
Abu Hanifa's proof: the impediment (mana') is the slave's debt and the deficiency in ownership. Sahibayn's proof: emancipation is indivisible, so freeing a share frees the whole, and the debt is merely a financial matter.
The disagreement hinges on whether 'itq is divisible.
5
Determine the tarjih
The relied-upon view in the Hanafi school is Abu Hanifa's, as stated in al-Bahr al-Ra'iq and al-Kafi. Therefore, the mufta bihi is that freeing a share of a jointly owned slave and guaranteeing the rest does not suffice for kaffara.
However, the Sahibayn's view is also considered, so a mufti may take it in cases of necessity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Key Terms
الضمان (al-daman): Guarantee; here, paying the value of the other partner's share to complete the slave's freedom.
العتق (al-'itq): Emancipation of a slave; in kaffara, it must be a complete, unencumbered slave.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the mufta bihi in the Hanafi school, freeing a half share of a jointly owned slave and guaranteeing the rest for kaffara is:
A) Valid if wealthy.
B) Valid only if the slave is freed completely immediately.
C) Not valid because of the debt encumbrance.
D) Valid because emancipation is indivisible.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school has an internal dispute on using a jointly owned slave for kaffara; the relied-upon view is Abu Hanifa's, rejecting it due to the slave's debt and deficiency in ownership.
